From the XRD patterns (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>), the characteristic peaks of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub> could be observed at the beginning of discharge process. Subsequently, the peak intensities gradually weakened and finally disappeared at 1.2 V, suggesting that both selenides have been converted into Na<sub>2</sub>Se, which is confirmed by the appearance of the two characteristic peaks at 22.5° and 37.3° of Na<sub>2</sub>Se. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> When fully discharged to 0 V, the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Se can still be observed, and two new peaks appearing at 33° and 43.7° could be indexed to InNa alloy and Co, respectively. The peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Se disappeared after charging to 2.0 V. Notably, when charged to 2.5 V, no obvious peak of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub> could be observed, indicating the poor crystallinity of these formed metal selenides after the sodiation/desodiation process.</div><div class="NLM_p"><i>Ex situ</i> XPS spectra were also taken at different states to provide in-depth information on the conversion process in In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). It is clear that both peaks in the In 3d spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a</a>) first exhibited a downshift to lower binding energies during discharge from 1.5 to 0 V and then an upper shift back to the higher energy levels during charge from 0 to 2.5 V, indicating the reversible conversion from In<sup>3+</sup> to In<sup>0</sup> during the discharge process, and then back to In<sup>3+</sup> during charge. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The situation is slightly more complicated for Co. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12b</a>, in both 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, the Co<sup>2+</sup> peaks displayed a similar shifting pattern as In<sup>3+</sup>, which is consistent with the conversion mechanism. At the same time, the peaks of Co<sup>3+</sup>, which originated from the surface oxidation, exhibited an upper shift to higher binding energy at the end of discharge to 0 V, indicating the conversion of Co<sup>3+</sup> to Co<sup>0</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> However, no downshift of the peak could be observed during the subsequent charging process and only a slight decrease in the peak intensity could be identified, suggesting that oxidation of Co<sup>0</sup> back to Co<sup>3+</sup> was difficult to take place but most likely to Co<sup>2+</sup> during the desodiation process. These <i>ex situ</i> characterization results are consistent with the proposed mechanism, and also in good agreement with the CV data (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a).</div><div class="NLM_p">Inspired by the electrochemical performance of the half-cell, we also accessed the potential of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 for practical application by evaluating the full-cell performance using Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) as the cathode. The cycling performance of the NVP cathode was first investigated (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>), displaying a reversible capacity of about 92.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.5 C (1C = 117 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). The typical galvanostatic discharge and charge curves of the full cell cycled between 0.2 and 3.0 V at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>g, where a high initial discharge capacity of about 498.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can be obtained. Remarkably, the full cell displays a quite stable cyclability after a rapid decay in the first 5 cycles and a high reversible capacity of 402.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating the promising potential for practical SIB application.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to further investigate the electrochemical kinetics of the as-prepared electrodes, the capacity contribution was studied by the CV analysis at various scan rates from 0.2 to 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 shows CV curves of similar shapes with only minor peak shifts as the scan rate increases. The relationship between current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) can be described using the equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq9"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/medium/nn1c03056_m009.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(9)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq10"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/medium/nn1c03056_m010.gif" alt="" id="_i12"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(10)</span></div></div></span>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable parameters and the <i>b</i> value represents the charge storage behavior, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> the range of which is typically between 0.5 and 1.0. When the electrochemical process is mainly controlled by ionic diffusion, <i>b</i> is close to 0.5; when the <i>b</i> value approaches 1.0, the process is dominated by a capacitive effect. The <i>b</i> values of the two peaks marked in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a were calculated to be 0.86 and 0.99 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b), suggesting that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 was predominantly controlled by a capacitive process. With the two different <i>b</i> values for the anodic and cathodic processes, it may be related to the reaction processes which hold different reaction mechanisms of active substances during discharge and charge. As the value of Peak 1 is closer to 0.5 than that of Peak 2, it implies that the electrochemical reaction represented by Peak 1 contained more ionic diffusion. Meanwhile, In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-400, pure In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, and CoSe<sub>2</sub> all showed relatively low <i>b</i> values (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>), indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process in these samples contained both diffusion and capacitive processes. In addition, the contribution of the capacitive effect can be quantified using the equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq11"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/medium/nn1c03056_m011.gif" alt="" id="_i13"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(11)</span></div></div></span>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup> represent the capacity and diffusion contributions, respectively. Accordingly, the capacitive contribution of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 is calculated to be 80.2% at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and increases to 93.3% at 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, with a quantitative analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>) that is analogous to other transition metal selenides.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/medium/nn1c03056_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/large/nn1c03056_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) CV curves at various scan rates, (b) corresponding log <i>i</i><i>versus</i> log <i>v</i> plot at each redox peak, and (c) normalized ratio of diffusion-controlled or capacitive contributions at various scan rates of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450. (d) Nyquist plots and (e) GITT curves and the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients for (I) In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450, (II) In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-400, (III) In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, and (IV) CoSe<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/large/nn1c03056_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c03056&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the dynamic process of the as-prepared samples during Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were conducted. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, the Nyquist plots of the four samples show similar profiles, which consist of a depressed semi-circle in the high-frequency region, which corresponds to the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), and an up-sloping line in the low-frequency region representing the Warburg diffusion process (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 55 and 56">(55,56)</a> The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 is calculated to be 15.7 Ω (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), which is much lower than that of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-400 (38.0 Ω), pure In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> (51.9 Ω), and CoSe<sub>2</sub> (67.3 Ω), implying the fastest charge transfer process in In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450. Additionally, the EIS measurements of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 after the 100th, 200th, and 500th cycles were also carried out (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>). The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values are comparable after 100 (7.6 Ω) and 200 (6.9 Ω) cycles, and slightly increased to 9.9 Ω after 500 cycles, indicating that a relatively low charge transfer resistance could be maintained even after the long cycling test, which further revealed a high stability in both the microscopic structure and electrochemical properties of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450.</div><div class="NLM_p">Meanwhile, the diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> in our electrodes were measured by the Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT) and could be calculated based on a simplified equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 57 and 58">(57,58)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq12"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/medium/nn1c03056_m012.gif" alt="" id="_i14"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(12)</span></div></div></span>where Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the potential change (V) due to the current pulse, Δ<i>E</i><sub>t</sub> stands for the potential change (V) during the galvanostatic discharge after eliminating the <i>i</i><sub>R</sub> drop, <i>L</i> is the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion length, which could be simply taken as the thickness of the electrode, and τ represents the relaxation time (s). The GITT curves and the corresponding <i>D</i> values of the discharge and charge processes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e. The diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>app.Na</sub>) of the four samples exhibit a similar variation trend, and the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 electrode shows the highest <i>D</i><sub>app.Na</sub> in the range between 1 × 10<sup>–11</sup> and 1 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>. Consistent with the above EIS analysis, these GITT results further revealed the fact that building the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub> heterostructure with two interfaces can effectively reduce the charge transfer resistance, accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> migration, and thus result in a superior rate performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the exact role of the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub> heterostructure in improving the Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties, density functional theory (DFT) calculation was employed to investigate the effects of the different interfaces. According to the TEM and XRD measurement, we constructed three different heterostructure models, where In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-400 consisted of only one interface of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, whereas the bilateral interfaces of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> were present in In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a displays the migration paths of Na at these three different interfaces. The Na diffusion energy barrier of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub> is 1.37 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b), which is apparently higher than that of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> (0.76 eV) and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> (0.57 eV), revealing a faster Na diffusion process in In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub>. Additionally, the adsorption energies of the Na atom (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c) on In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> (−2.66 eV) and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> (−1.56 eV) are higher than that on In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub> (−1.44 eV), suggesting more stable adsorption in the formers, which is mainly due to the presence of the CoIn<sub>2</sub> phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/medium/nn1c03056_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/large/nn1c03056_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Na migration paths and (b) corresponding energy barriers in the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub>, CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub>, and the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub> heterostructured interfaces. (c) Na adsorption energies, (d) calculated DOS, and (e) charge density difference of the three different models. The yellow and blue clouds represent the negative and positive charge differences, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/large/nn1c03056_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c03056&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Meanwhile, in the calculated density of states (DOS) of these models, both pure In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub> displayed an obvious band gap of 0.91 and 1.3 eV, respectively, suggesting relatively low intrinsic electrical conductivities (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>). After constructing heterostructures, In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub> still possesses a significant band gap (0.3 eV), whereas In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> exhibit no band gaps and an enhanced electron density near the Fermi level (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60 ref61" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60 ref61" aria-label="reference 60 and 61">(60,61)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Considering that this phenomenon might be caused by the electron transfer between different phases, the work functions (<i>E</i><sub>w</sub>) of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, CoIn<sub>2</sub>, and CoSe<sub>2</sub> were calculated, and the corresponding energy distribution curves are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>. The <i>E</i><sub>w</sub> can be derived by the following equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq13"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/medium/nn1c03056_m013.gif" alt="" id="_i15"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(13)</span></div></div></span>where <i>E</i><sub>vac</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>f</sub> represent the vacuum layer level and Fermi level, respectively, which are marked as dashed platforms in the figures. The CoIn<sub>2</sub> exerts a higher <i>E</i><sub>w</sub> (6.34 eV) than In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> (5.51 eV) and CoSe<sub>2</sub> (5.24 eV), indicating that the free electrons could flow from CoIn<sub>2</sub> to In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub> at the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> interfaces, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> which would promote the establishment of internal electric fields in these heterostructures, thus reducing the electrical conductivity and accelerating Na<sup>+</sup> migration during the cycling process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> In contrast, the difference in the <i>E</i><sub>w</sub> values of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub> is very trivial, revealing a low charge transfer efficiency between these two phases. Such a strong tendency in charge transfer is further illustrated by the charge density differences of these models (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e), where more significant charge accumulation/depletion could be clearly observed at the interfaces of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> than In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub>. Collectively, we could conclude the two possible reasons for In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 to show a higher capacity than In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-400: (1) Some binary alloys containing both active and inactive metals still possess good sodium storage properties, such as Sn-Co <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> and Sn-Ni, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> where Co and Ni are inactive, whereas Sn is active. As a result, the In component in the current CoIn<sub>2</sub> alloy should still be able to react with Na at the specific potential. (2) As verified by the DFT calculations, the bilateral heterointerfaces (In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub>) in In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 significantly enhanced the kinetic process of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and electrical conduction, leading to a more efficient reaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and the active materials during the charge and discharge processes, thus higher reversible capacities.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub> heterostructure with In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> bilateral interfaces was successfully constructed in MOF-derived hollow nanorods. The CoIn<sub>2</sub> alloy phase could be introduced into the composite by tuning the selenization temperature, leading to a strong coupling between the In and Co atoms. The DFT results suggested that CoIn<sub>2</sub> can not only enhance the adsorption and reduce the diffusion barrier of Na atoms but also optimize the electronic structure <i>via</i> spontaneous electron transfer, resulting in a smaller charge transfer resistance and faster Na<sup>+</sup> migration at the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub>-CoIn<sub>2</sub> interfaces than the CoIn<sub>2</sub>-free In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub> counterpart. As a result, the sample with the bilateral interfaces demonstrated extraordinary sodium storage capabilities with 297.5 and 205.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles, respectively, and an excellent rate performance of 371.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Such sodium storage properties are significantly better than the sample with the single In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>-CoSe<sub>2</sub> interface, phase-pure In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, and CoSe<sub>2</sub>, and also other selenide-based anodes. Our work presented an innovative strategy to construct advanced heterostructures with multiple interfaces, which can be applied for the design of high-performance materials for electrochemical storage systems.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i17">Methods/Experimental</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i18" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i18"> Synthesis of Hexagonal MIL-68 Prisms</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The MIL-68 prisms were synthesized according to a reported solvothermal method. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> Typically, 60 mg of In(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·<i>x</i>H<sub>2</sub>O (Aladdin) and 60 mg of 1,4-benzenedicarboxylic acid were dissolved in 40 mL of DMF. Then, the resultant solution was placed in an oil bath at 120 °C for 30 min. After the temperature was cooled down to room temperature, the white precipitate was filtrated and washed with ethanol three times.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i19" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i19"> Synthesis of MIL-68@ZIF-67 Nanorods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The growth of ZIF-67 polyhedrons on the surface of the MIL-68 prisms was achieved at room temperature. Typically, 0.1 g of the as-obtained MIL-68 prisms and 0.2 g of Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved in 20 mL of methanol under ultrasonic treatment. Subsequently, 20 mL of methanol containing 0.43 g of 2-methylimidazole and 0.75 g of PVP (10000) was slowly dripped into the above-mentioned mixture with stirring. The mixture was then stirred for 30 min at room temperature. The purple product was collected by centrifuging and washing with ethanol and water several times, followed by drying at 60 °C for overnight.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i20" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i20"> Synthesis of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub> Nanorods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The MIL-68@ZIF-67 precursor was first carbonized at 600 °C for 2 h under an argon flow at a ramping rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. Then the In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub> nanorods were obtained by thermal selenization. Briefly, the carbonized MIL-68@ZIF-67 and an appropriate amount of Se powder were put at the downstream and upstream positions in one ceramic boat, respectively, and heated for 8 h at 400 °C (denoted as In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-400) and 450 °C (denoted as In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450).</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i21" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i21"> Synthesis of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> Nanorod</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The MIL-68 precursor was first carbonized at 600 °C for 2 h under an argon flow at a ramping rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup> and then annealing with Se powder at 400 °C for 8 h to obtain In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i22" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i22"> Synthesis of CoSe<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical synthesis, solution A was prepared by dissolving 0.2 g of Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O into 20 mL of methanol and solution B was prepared by dissolving 0.43 g of 2-methylimidazole in another 20 mL of methanol. Afterward, the two solutions were mixed rapidly and stirred for 30 min at room temperature. After being washed with ethanol and dried, the ZIF-67 precursor was first carbonized at 600 °C for 2 h under an argon flow at a ramping rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup> and then annealed with Se powder at 400 °C for 8 h to obtain CoSe<sub>2</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec4_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i23" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i23"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology and nanostructure were analyzed by field emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM; Phenom Pharos) and transmission electronic microscopy (TEM; JEM2010F). The crystal structures were defined by an X-ray diffraction instrument (XRD, Bruker D8 Advanced) equipped with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.54 Å) in the 2θ range from 5° to 80° with an accelerating voltage and current of 40 kV and 40 mA. Raman spectroscopy was performed on a Thermo Fisher Scientific DXR Raman spectrometer with an excited wavelength of 532 nm in the wavenumber range of 900–2100 cm<sup>–1</sup>. Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis was carried out using a TGA/DSC thermal analyzer (NETZSCHSTA 449 C) in air with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> from room temperature to 800 °C to determine the content of carbon. The nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms and pore size distribution were conducted on a surface analyzer (Kubo-X1000; Builder), and the specific surface area and pore size distribution were obtained <i>via</i> the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method and Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) model. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Scientific K-Alpha) was used to discuss the chemical state of the product.</div></div><div id="sec4_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i24" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i24"> Electrochemical Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p last">CR2032 coin-type cells were used to test the sodium storage performance. The working electrodes used Cu foil as the current collector and consist of the active materials (In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450, In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-400, pure In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, and CoSe<sub>2</sub>), Super P carbon black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) at the weight ratio of 7:2:1, and the mass loading of active materials is controlled at about 1 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Na metal was used as the counter and reference electrodes, and glass fiber (Whatman) was used as the separator film. 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in dimethyl ether (DME) was used as the separator and the electrolyte. Galvanostatic charge-discharge tests were conducted at a voltage window of 0.01–2.5 V. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was measured at different scan rates, and electrochemical impedance spectrum (EIS) measurements were investigated in the frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.1 Hz. For the full cell test, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode and In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>-450 anodes were paired at a weight ratio of 8:1 and tested at a voltage window of 0.2–3 V.</div></div><div id="sec4_8" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i25" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i25"> Theoretical Calculation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">First-principles calculations were performed <i>via</i> the Density Functional Theory (DFT) method coupled with the Vienna <i>Ab initio</i> Simulation Package (VASP, University of Vienna, Austria). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66 ref67" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref66 ref67" aria-label="reference 66 and 67">(66,67)</a> The generalized gradient approximation (GGA) in the formulation of Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) was used to treat the exchange and correlation energy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a> The cutoff energy of 450 eV was adopted for the wave basis sets. A <i>k</i>-points sampling with 0.04 and 0.08 Å<sup>–1</sup> separation was used in the Brillouin zone for geometry optimization and density of states, respectively. The force and energy were converged to 0.02 eV Å<sup>–1</sup> and 2.0 × 10<sup>–5</sup> eV, respectively. The cutoff energy of 450 eV was set for the plane wave basis. The (001), (010), and (011) planes of In<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>, CoSe<sub>2</sub>, and CoIn<sub>2</sub> were adopted, respectively, to construct heterointerfaces. Moreover, a 1 × 3 supercell was built in this work. The vacuum layer thickness of 20 Å was applied to avoid virtual interaction. The adsorption energy of Na (Δ<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>) was defined as<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/asset/images/medium/nn1c03056_m014.gif" alt="" id="_i26"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>where <i>E</i><sub>total</sub> is the total energy of the substrate adsorbed with Na atom, while <i>E</i><sub>base</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> are the energy of the clean substrate and Na atom, respectively.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i27">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Details of experimental procedures, materials characterization, electrochemical measurement, and theoretical calculation; additional SEM, TEM, TGA, XRD, and Raman data; <i>ex situ</i> XRD patterns and XPS spectra; more electrochemical data such as CV, EIS, discharge-charge curves, and cycling performance at low current density; theoretical calculation data including DOS and energy distribution curves; comparison of the electrochemical performances of anodes for various reported SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c03056/suppl_file/nn1c03056_si_001.pdf">nn1c03056_si_001.pdf (2.1 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.1c03056" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Access to clean, affordable and reliable energy has been a cornerstone of the world's increasing prosperity and economic growth since the beginning of the industrial revolution.  Our use of energy in the twenty-first century must also be sustainable.  Solar and water-based energy generation, and engineering of microbes to produce biofuels are a few examples of the alternatives.  This Perspective puts these opportunities into a larger context by relating them to a no. of aspects in the transportation and electricity generation sectors.  It also provides a snapshot of the current energy landscape and discusses several research and development opportunities and pathways that could lead to a prosperous, sustainable and secure energy future for the world.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/s41586-018-0347-0"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Griffith, K.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Niobium tungsten oxides for high-rate lithium-ion energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Griffith, Kent J.; Wiaderek, Kamila M.; Cibin, Giannantonio; Marbella, Lauren E.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature (London, United Kingdom)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">559</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7715</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">556-563</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NATUAS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0028-0836</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The max. power output and min. charging time of a lithium-ion battery depend on both ionic and electronic transport.  Ionic diffusion within the electrochem. active particles generally represents a fundamental limitation to the rate at which a battery can be charged and discharged.  To compensate for the relatively slow solid-state ionic diffusion and to enable high power and rapid charging, the active particles are frequently reduced to nanometer dimensions, to the detriment of volumetric packing d., cost, stability and sustainability.  As an alternative to nanoscaling, here we show that two complex niobium tungsten oxides-Nb16W5O55 and Nb18W16O93, which adopt crystallog. shear and bronze-like structures, resp.-can intercalate large quantities of lithium at high rates, even when the sizes of the niobium tungsten oxide particles are of the order of micrometres.  Measurements of lithium-ion diffusion coeffs. in both structures reveal room-temp. values that are several orders of magnitude higher than those in typical electrode materials such as Li4Ti5O12 and LiMn2O4.  Multielectron redox, buffered vol. expansion, topol. frustrated niobium/tungsten polyhedral arrangements and rapid solid-state lithium transport lead to extremely high volumetric capacities and rate performance.  Unconventional materials and mechanisms that enable lithiation of micrometre-sized particles in minutes have implications for high-power applications, fast-charging devices, all-solid-state energy storage systems, electrode design and material discovery.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1126/science.1212741"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dunn, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kamath, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrical Energy Storage for the Grid: A Battery of Choices</span>. <i>Science</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>334</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">928</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">935</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201703772"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nayak, P.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">From Lithium-Ion to Sodium-Ion Batteries: Advantages, Challenges, and Surprises</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nayak, Prasant Kumar; Yang, Liangtao; Brehm, Wolfgang; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">57</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">102-120</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mobile and stationary energy storage by rechargeable batteries is a topic of broad societal and economical relevance.  Lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. is at the forefront of the development, but a massively growing market will likely put severe pressure on resources and supply chains.  Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been reconsidered with the aim of providing a lower-cost alternative that is less susceptible to resource and supply risks.  On paper, the replacement of lithium by sodium in a battery seems straightforward at first, but unpredictable surprises are often found in practice.  What happens when replacing lithium by sodium in electrode reactions.  This review provides a state-of-the art overview on the redox behavior of materials when used as electrodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries, resp.  Advantages and challenges related to the use of sodium instead of lithium are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5033"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsuda, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishii, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cumings, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Expanded Graphite as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                 10.1038/ncomms5033</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D4033%26journal%3DNat.%2BCommun.%26author%3DY.%2BWen%26author%3DK.%2BHe%26author%3DY.%2BZhu%26author%3DF.%2BHan%26author%3DY.%2BXu%26author%3DI.%2BMatsuda%26author%3DY.%2BIshii%26author%3DJ.%2BCumings%26author%3DC.%2BWang%26title%3DExpanded%2BGraphite%2Bas%2BSuperior%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fncomms5033&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.1c03056&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fncomms5033&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, Yang; He, Kai; Zhu, Yujie; Han, Fudong; Xu, Yunhua; Matsuda, Isamu; Ishii, Yoshitaka; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4033</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201901351"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Extended “Adsorption-Insertion” Model: A New Insight into the Sodium Storage Mechanism of Hard Carbons</span>. <i>Adv.                 10.1038/nnano.2015.194</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D10%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D980-5%26journal%3DNat.%2BNanotechnol.%26author%3DJ.%2BSun%26author%3DH.%2BW.%2BLee%26author%3DM.%2BPasta%26author%3DH.%2BYuan%26author%3DG.%2BZheng%26author%3DY.%2BSun%26author%3DY.%2BLi%26author%3DY.%2BCui%26title%3DA%2BPhosphorene-Graphene%2BHybrid%2BMaterial%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Capacity%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fnnano.2015.194&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.1c03056&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fnnano.2015.194&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A phosphorene-graphene hybrid material as a high-capacity anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Pasta, Mauro; Yuan, Hongtao; Zheng, Guangyuan; Sun, Yongming; Li, Yuzhang; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">980-985</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have recently attracted significant attention as an alternative to Li-ion batteries because Na sources do not present the geopolitical issues that Li sources might.  Although recent reports on cathode materials for Na-ion batteries demonstrated performances comparable to their Li-ion counterparts, the major scientific challenge for a competitive Na-ion battery technol. is to develop viable anode materials.  A hybrid material made out of a few phosphorene layers sandwiched between graphene layers shows a specific capacity of 2 440 mA h g-1 (calcd. using the mass of P only) at a c.d. of 0.05 A g-1 and an 83% capacity retention after 100 cycles while operating between 0 and 1.5 V.  Using in situ TEM and ex situ x-ray diffraction techniques, the authors explain the large capacity of the anode through a dual mechanism of intercalation of Na ions along the x axis of the phosphorene layers followed by the formation of a Na3P alloy.  The presence of graphene layers in the hybrid material works as a mech. backbone and an elec. highway, ensuring that a suitable elastic buffer space accommodates the anisotropic expansion of phosphorene layers along the y and z axial directions for stable cycling operation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201908665"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bayaguud, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advantageous Functional Integration of AdsorptionIntercalation-Conversion Hybrid Mechanisms in 3D Flexible Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>@Hard Carbon@MoS<sub>2</sub>@Soft Carbon Fiber Paper Anodes for Ultrafast and Super-Stable Sodium Storage</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advantageous Functional Integration of Adsorption-Intercalation-Conversion Hybrid Mechanisms in 3D Flexible Nb2O5@Hard Carbon@MoS2@Soft Carbon Fiber Paper Anodes for Ultrafast and Super-Stable Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Deng, Qinglin; Chen, Feng; Liu, Si; Bayaguud, Aruuhan; Feng, Yuezhan; Zhang, Zhibo; Fu, Yanpeng; Yu, Yan; Zhu, Changbao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Using synergetic effects of various sodium storage modes and materials to construct high power, high energy, and long cycling flexible sodium anode materials is significant and still challenging.  Here, by advantageous functional integration of adsorption-intercalation-conversion sodium storage mechanisms, a 3D flexible fiber paper anode with the compn. of Nb2O5@hard carbon@MoS2@soft carbon is designed and prepd.  Based on the synergetic effects, it exhibits higher specific capacity than pure Nb2O5, with more excellent rate performance (245, 201, 155, 133, and 97 mAh g-1 at the c.d. of 0.2, 1, 5, 10, and 20 A g-1, resp.) than pure MoS2 as well as admirable long-term cycling characteristics (≈82% capacity retention after 20 000 cycles at 5 A g-1).  Relevant kinetics mechanisms are expounded in detail.  This work can be helpful for prepg. other types of hybrid and flexible electrodes for energy storage systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2019.06.015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kong, H.                 <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hybrid materials, integrating the merits of individual components, are ideal structures for efficient sodium storage.  However, the construction of hybrid structures with decent phys./electrochem. properties is still challenging.  Now, the elaborate design and synthesis of hierarchical nanoboxes composed of three-layered Cu2S@carbon@MoS2 as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries is reported.  Through a facile multistep template-engaged strategy, ultrathin MoS2 nanosheets are grown on nitrogen-doped carbon-coated Cu2S nanoboxes to realize the Cu2S@carbon@MoS2 configuration.  The design shortens the diffusion path of electrons/Na+ ions, accommodates the vol. change of electrodes during cycling, enhances the elec. cond. of the hybrids, and offers abundant active sites for sodium uptake.  By virtue of these advantages, these three-layered Cu2S@carbon@MoS2 hierarchical nanoboxes show excellent electrochem. properties in terms of decent rate capability and stable cycle life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2020.04.004"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sa, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rational Construction of Heterostructured Core-Shell Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> Complex Hollow Particles toward High-Performance Li- and Na-Ion Storage</span>. <i>Energy Storage Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>29</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">121</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">130</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Se-C Bonding Promoting Fast and Durable Na+ Storage in Yolk-Shell SnSe2@Se-C</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Shuhao; Li, Zhenzhe; Liu, Jintao; Song, Yushan; Li, Tingshuai; Xiang, Yong; Chen, Jun Song; Yan, Qingyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">41</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin-based compds. have received much attention as anode materials for lithium/sodium ion batteries owing to their high theor. capacity.  However, the huge vol. change usually leads to the pulverization of electrode, giving rise to a poor cycle performance, which have severely hampered their practical application.  Herein, highly durable yolk-shell SnSe2 nanospheres (SnSe2@Se-C) are prepd. by a multistep templating method, with an in situ gas-phase selenization of the SnO2@C hollow nanospheres.  During this process, Se can be doped into the carbon shell with a tunable amt. and form Se-C bonds.  D. functional theory calcn. results reveal that the Se-C bonding can enhance the charge transfer properties as well as the binding interaction between the SnSe2 core and the carbon shell, favoring an improved rate performance and a superior cyclability.  As expected, the sample delivers reversible capacities of 441 and 406 mAh g-1 after 2000 cycles at 2 and 5 A g-1, resp., as the anode material for a sodium-ion battery.  Such performances are significantly better than the control sample without the Se-C bonding and also other metal selenide-based anodes, evidently showing the advantage of Se doping in the carbon shell.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201805552"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Metal selenides for high performance sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Minghe; Yu, Haoxiang; Hu, Feiyang; Liu, Tingting; Cheng, Xing; Zheng, Runtian; Bai, Ying; Shui, Miao; Shu, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">380</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">122557</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is given.  In consideration of the abundance of Na resources, Na ion batteries (SIBs) have been revisited recently and are considered as a substitution for Li ion batteries (LIBs).  Among all the proposed anodes for SIBs, metal selenides labeled as high theor. capacity materials have aroused the interest of battery researchers.  However, as conversion/alloying based electrode materials, metal selenides suffer from a severe vol. change during cycling, thus leading to kinetic problems and poor electrochem. stability, which restrain their further application in SIBs.  Therefore, modification strategies such as coupling with carbonaceous material, designing unique structure, selecting an upper cut-off voltage, optimizing the compn. of electrolyte, controlling the compn. of the material etc. have been adopted to alleviate the vol. change issue of metal selenides electrodes.  The research progresses on the metal selenides as electrodes for SIBs are outlined and the difference and necessity of each metal selenide from the perspectives of structure and cond. are emphasized.  The summarization of metal selenides contains the synthesis methods, modification methods for performance improvement, corresponding reaction mechanism and performance in full-cell system.  A conclusion with the challenges and outlook of metal selenides in the field of SIBs is also presented.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201602608"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, M.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Urchin-Like CoSe2 as a High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Kai; Park, Mihui; Zhou, Limin; Lee, Gi-Hyeok; Li, Weijie; Kang, Yong-Mook; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Urchin-like CoSe2 assembled by nanorods was synthesized via simple solvothermal route and was 1st applied as an anode material for Na-ion batteries (SIBs) with ether-based electrolytes.  The CoSe2 delivers excellent sodiation and de-sodiation properties when using 1 M NaCF3SO3 in diethyleneglycol dimethylether as an electrolyte and cycling between 0.5 and 3.0 V.  A high discharge capacity of 0.410 A h g-1 is obtained at 1 A g-1 after 1800 cycles, corresponding to a capacity retention of 98.6% calcd. from the 30th cycle.  Even at an ultrahigh rate of 50 A g-1, the capacity still maintains 0.097 A h g-1.  The reaction mechanism of the as-prepd. CoSe2 is also studied.  At discharged 1.56 V, insertion reaction occurs, while 2 conversion reactions take place at the 2nd and 3rd plateaus around 0.98 and 0.65 V.  During the charge process, Co 1st reacts with Na2Se to form NaxCoSe2 and then turns back to CoSe2.  In addn. to Na/CoSe2 half cells, Na3V2(PO4)3/CoSe2 full cell with excessive amt. of Na3V2(PO4)3 was studied.  The full cell exhibits a reversible capacity of 0.380 Ah g-1.  This work enriches the possibilities for anode materials for SIBs with high performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201706668"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Long cycle life and high rate capability of three dimensional CoSe2 grain-attached carbon nanofibers for flexible sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yin, Hong; Qu, Hong-Qing; Liu, Zhitian; Jiang, Ren-Zhi; Li, Chong; Zhu, Ming-Qiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The flexible binderless films composed of cobalt selenide grains attached on carbon nanofibers (denoted as CoSe2/CNFs) are prepd. by electrospinning to enhance the electronic cond., Na-ions diffusion and alleviate vol. variation during charge/discharge process for high performance sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  The binderless and flexible CoSe2/CNFs electrode can obtain a high reversible capacity of 430 mAh g-1 after 400 cycles at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1, an exceptional rate capability of 224 mAh g-1 at 15 A g-1, even a capacity of 370 mAh g-1 at 2 A g-1 after 1000 cycles.  The superior electrochem. performance can be ascribed to the synergistic effects of the homogeneous distribution of CoSe2 grains attached to the carbon nanofibers and the high electronic cond. framework of 3D interconnecting carbon nanofibers, which can alleviate the vol. variation during charge-discharge process and sustain the high elec. cond. and fast Na-ions diffusion throughout the electrode.  This binderless flexible CoSe2/CNFs nanocomposite electrode may hold great application promising for high cycling performance SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2019.122206"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, H.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of CoSe2 nanoparticles embedded in N-doped carbon with conformal TiO2 shell for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Bo; Liu, Qianqian; Wei, Guijuan; Wang, Jianghao; Yu, Xin-Yao; Li, Xiang; Wu, Hao Bin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">CoSe2 is a potential anode material for Na-ion batteries in terms of their high Na storage capacity and tunable properties.  However, structural degrdn. and unstable interphase during the reversible sodiation and de-sodiation lead to fast capacity decay.  The authors develop a nanocomposite architecture based on CoSe2 nanoparticles embedded in N-doped C matrix with conformal TiO2 coating (CoSe2@NC@TiO2) by using a metal-org. framework-assisted strategy.  The small CoSe2 nanoparticles and N-doped C matrix facilitate charge transport and suppress the structural degrdn., while the redox-active TiO2 shell further strengthens the structural and interfacial stability without decreasing the capacity.  The as-prepd. CoSe2@NC@TiO2 nanocomposite particles deliver a reversible Na storage capacity of 520 mA-h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 with a capacity retention of 78% after 200 cycles, in contrast to the rapid capacity fading after 50 cycles of the pristine CoSe2@NC particles without TiO2 shell.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2019.03.263"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, S.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Mesoporous CoSe2 nanoclusters threaded with nitrogen-doped carbon nanotubes for high-performance sodium-ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Su Hyun; Park, Seung-Keun; Kang, Yun Chan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Currently, a great challenge to the design of durable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is the need for the architecture of nanostructured transition metal-selenide electrodes with high capacity and excellent cycling stability.  In this paper, a novel metal-org. framework (MOF)-induced approach is described to construct necklace-like carbon nanotube (CNT)-CoSe2@N-doped carbon (NC) with excellent sodium ion storage performance.  In this strategy, CNT-threaded zeolitic imidazolate framework-67 (ZIF-67) polyhedra, synthesized by wet chem. methods, are used as the precursor.  During the selenization step, ZIF-67 polyhedra transform into mesoporous nanoclusters consisted of CoSe2@NC nanoparticles, forming CNT-CoSe2@NC composites with a necklace-like morphol.  Such structures facilitate ion and electron transport, and inhibit the aggregation and pulverization of active materials during cycling processes via the intimate contact between the CNTs and CoSe2@NC.  The as-designed composites show significantly improved electrochem. properties including high discharge capacity (404 mA·h·g-1 after 120 cycles at 0.2 A·g-1), excellent rate performance (363 mA·h·g-1 at 5.0 A·g-1), and reasonable capacity retention (80% when calcd. from the 2nd cycle).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202000741"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Heterostructure SnSe<sub>2</sub>/ZnSe@PDA Nanobox for Stable and Highly Efficient Sodium-Ion Storage</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Heterostructure SnSe2/ZnSe@PDA Nanobox for Stable and Highly Efficient Sodium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Pei; Han, Jun; Zhu, Kunjie; Dong, Zihao; Jiao, Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

                        cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Construction of ultrafine ZnSe nanoparticles on/in amorphous carbon hollow nanospheres with high-power-density sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Shiyao; Zhu, Tianxiang; Wu, Hu; Wang, Yuankun; Li, Jiao; Abdelkader, Amr; Xi, Kai; Wang, Wei; Li, Yanguang; Ding, Shujiang; Gao, Guoxin; Kumar, R. Vasant</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as a promising candidate to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) owing to the inexpensive and abundant sodium reserves.  However, the application of anode materials for SIBs still confront rapid capacity fading and undesirable rate capability.  Here we simultaneously grow ultrafine ZnSe nanoparticles on the inner walls and the outer surface of hollow carbon nanospheres (ZnSe@HCNs), giving a unique hierarchical hybrid nanostructure that can sustain a capacity of 361.9 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1 over 1000 cycles and 266.5 mAh g-1 at 20 A g-1. Also, a full cell is constructed from our designed ZnSe@HCNs anode and Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode.  It delivers a reversible discharge capacity of about 313.1 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g-1 with high Columbic efficiency over 98.2%.  The outstanding sodium storage of as-prepd. ZnSe@HCNs is attributed to the confinement of ZnSe structural changes both inside/outside of hollow nanospheres during the sodiation/desodiation processes.
